[2025-11-03T00:19:59Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: South Africa. . Its national anthem starts with Nkosi Sikelele iAfrika, a Xhosa hymn that means "God bless Africa." In translation, it was adopted as the national anthem by Zimbabwe, Zambia, Tanzania and Namibia. In the apartheid years, the hymn Nkosi Sikelele became a symbol of protest against the regime. When Mandela became president, it acquired joint status as one of the two national anthems, alongside the old apartheid anthem Die Stem van Suid Afrika ("The Call of South Africa"). Later, a single anthem was created incorporating verses in Xhosa, the Zulu translation of Nkosi Sikelele, Sotho, Afrikaans and English. The original hymn in translation is still the anthem of Zambia and Tanzania. Zimbabwe and Namibia have since changed their anthems.

[2025-11-04T03:37:46Z] Pavan Pamidimarri: This regiment of the Indian Army traces its origins to the East India Company’s wars with Tipu Sultan of Mysore. In the mid-1790s, the Nizam of Hyderabad employed French officers to command his troops. The Company disbanded those units and reorganized them into the Nizam’s Contingent under British officers. This unit participated in the decisive Battle of Srirangapatnam in 1799. It was renamed the Hyderabad Contingent in 1853 and over time became the 19th Hyderabad Regiment. Most of the troops, however, were from a hilly area in the north of India. In recognition of this, the regiment was renamed in 1945 as the 19th X Regiment. After independence, it became known as the X Regiment, dropping the regiment numbering of the British Indian Army. This regiment has produced three Chiefs of Army Staff, including Gen. Thimayya from the previous question. The regiment has won two Param Vir Chakras, including the first ever awarded to Maj. Somnath Sharma of the regiment’s 4th battalion. ID X.
